    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="4e24580d74314322a236984339cfc275">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '67a6466c-235a-430f-b148-6dd5e375cd5f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d256563355" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Based on multielectron conversion reactions, layered transition metal dichalcogenides are considered promising electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries, but suffer from poor cycling performance and rate capability due to their low intrinsic conductivity and severe volume variations. Here, interlayer-expanded MoSe<sub>2</sub>/phosphorus-doped carbon hybrid nanospheres coated by anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (denoted as MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>) are prepared by a facile hydrolysis reaction, in which TiO<sub>2</sub> coating polypyrrole-phosphomolybdic acid is utilized as a novel precursor followed by a selenization process. Benefiting from synergistic effects of MoSe<sub>2</sub>, phosphorus-doped carbon, and TiO<sub>2</sub>, the hybrid nanospheres manifest unprecedented cycling stability and ultrafast pseudocapacitive sodium storage capability. The MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> delivers decent reversible capacities of 214 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 8000 cycles, 154 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 10000 cycles, and an exceptional rate capability up to 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity of ≈175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in a voltage range of 0.5–3.0 V. Coupled with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C cathode, a full cell successfully confirms a reversible capacity of 242.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles with a coulombic efficiency over 99%.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs876-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs876-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) possessing a similar structure characteristics and energy storage mechanism to those of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), serving as one of the most promising energy-storage devices, have attracted our tremendous attention on account of their low cost, abundant, and widespread sodium resources.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0001_R_d256563257e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nano Energy">1</a>-<a href="#advs876-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0004_R_d256563257e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></span> Nevertheless, the large ionic radius of an Na<sup>+</sup> ion (≈1.09 Å), which is 55% larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup> ion, and its higher molar mass make the sodiation/desodiation kinetics more sluggish, impeding the development and practical application of SIBs.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0005_R_d256563257e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#advs876-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0007_R_d256563257e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Nano">7</a></span> For these reasons, numerous effort including development of appropriate anode materials has been taken to satisfy the demands of decent specific capacity, long cycle life, and excellent rate performance for stationary energy storage.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0008_R_d256563257e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small">8</a>-<a href="#advs876-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0010_R_d256563257e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></span></p>
         
         <p>MoSe<sub>2</sub>, a representative type of layered transition metal dichalcogenide (TMD) material, exhibits high theoretical capacity, large interlayer spacing, and small bandgap, and thus has been proved to be a good candidate anode material for SIBs.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0011_R_d256563257e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>-<a href="#advs876-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0015_R_d256563257e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nanoscale">15</a></span> Unfortunately, it still suffers from rapid cycling capacity degradation caused by large volume change and slow kinetics during the sodiation/desodiation process. According to previous research,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0016_R_d256563257e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. - Eur. J.">16</a>-<a href="#advs876-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0019_R_d256563257e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a></span> construction of rational nanostructures and formation of carbon-based composites are effective approaches. Various nanostructures, including MoSe<sub>2</sub>@porous hollow carbon spheres,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0020_R_d256563257e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nanoscale">20</a></span> carbon-stabilized interlayer-expanded MoSe<sub>2</sub> nanosheets,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0021_R_d256563257e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">21</a></span> and fullerene-like MoSe<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles-embedded CNT balls,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0022_R_d256563257e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nanoscale">22</a></span> have been explored to enhance the electrochemical performance of MoSe<sub>2</sub>. On the other hand, the interlayer-expanded structures are very critical, which can provide efficient ion migration channels, facilitating to enhance charge/discharge kinetics. Sun's group successfully synthesized interlayer-expanded MoSe<sub>2</sub>@hollow carbon nanosphere (HCNS) materials, which exhibited a specific capacity of 471 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 3.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0023_R_d256563257e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></span> These results confirm that sodium storage performance can be enhanced through rational nanostructure engineering. However, in view of the long cycle life for practical applications, the serious volume changes during charge and discharge processes remain challenging and need to be further alleviated.</p>
         
         <p>As one of the typical insertion-type electrode materials for secondary batteries, TiO<sub>2</sub> possesses reasonable insertion potential and negligible change in volume, showing good structural stability and long cycle life.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0024_R_d256563257e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a>-<a href="#advs876-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0029_R_d256563257e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></span> Although its theoretical specific capacity is fairly low, it can serve as an effective protective layer for other electrode materials with severe volume expansion, such as metal oxides or metal sulfides through smart hybridization.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0030_R_d256563257e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a>-<a href="#advs876-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0035_R_d256563257e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. - Eur. J.">35</a></span> Such an approach can effectively ensure the specific capacity and achieve a long cycle life attributed to that the rigid TiO<sub>2</sub> layer could tolerate the interior volume variation of metal oxides or metal sulfides. For instance, Wang and co-workers reported porous hollow α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> core–shell nanospheres.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0030_R_d256563257e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></span> When used as anode materials in SIBs, these hybrid hierarchical nanospheres delivered a high reversible capacity of 267 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> with an excellent cycling stability up to 300 cycles.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we report an elaborate anode, consisted of interlayer-expanded MoSe<sub>2</sub>/phosphorus-doped carbon nanospheres composite and anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> coating (denoted as MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>). In a facile hydrolysis reaction in acidic medium, TiO<sub>2</sub> layer was coated on PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor nanospheres, followed by a confined selenization process, MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres can be obtained by inheriting the morphology of the PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor. Compared to previous reports on MoSe<sub>2</sub>-based composite electrodes, our MoSe<sub>2</sub>-based hybrid has the following advantages: (i) the phosphorus-doped carbon transformed from the PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor, can increase the conductivity of nanocomposite and provide more active sites between Na<sup>+</sup> and the electrolyte to improve the charge transfer efficiency; (ii) the expanded interlayer spacing of MoSe<sub>2</sub> can decrease the ion diffusion resistance and facilitate the fast Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction reaction kinetics; (iii) the coated TiO<sub>2</sub> layer plays an essential role in alleviating the volume changes upon sodiation/desodiation process, thus preserving the electrode integrity; and (iv) the synergistic effect of MoSe<sub>2</sub>, phosphorus-doped carbon, and TiO<sub>2</sub> can enhance the electrochemical performance for SIBs. Benefiting from the unique configuration of the as-fabricated MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres, when used as an anode material for SIBs, it exhibits ultralong cycle stability, superior specific capacity, and exceptional rate capability. Additionally, this hybrid structure manifests a relatively small irreversible capacity loss with a high initial columbic efficiency (ICE) of 81.3%, which further enable it a feasible anode for practical full cells.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs876-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs876-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthesis process of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres is schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs876-fig-0001">1</a>A. Briefly, the uniform PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> nanospheres are first synthesized through a polymerization reaction of phosphomolybdic acid and pyrrole forming polypyrrole-phosphomolybdic acid (PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub>). Then a layer of TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles is grown on the PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> nanospheres through a deposition process in chemical bath. Finally, MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres are successfully obtained after calcination with Se powder in a tube furnace. To estimate the influence of P-C and TiO<sub>2</sub> coating, MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C and MoSe<sub>2</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> composites were also prepared following a similar process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs876-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/df3a3aa8-0687-4c13-998c-d7af486a2e87/advs876-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/df3a3aa8-0687-4c13-998c-d7af486a2e87/advs876-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/73d9a57d-edeb-42dd-bc21-b10f41e1f6c2/advs876-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/df3a3aa8-0687-4c13-998c-d7af486a2e87/advs876-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs876-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">A) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process for the spherical MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocomposite. B) XRD patterns. C) Raman spectra. D) TGA curves. E) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid nanospheres and MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C nanospheres.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0001">1</a>B shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of as-obtained MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres. The diffraction peaks at 25.28°, 37.80°, 48.05°, and 62.68° can be assigned to (101), (004), (200), and (204) planes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 21-1272). Three characteristic peaks at 31.42°, 37.88°, and 55.92° are consistent with the planes of (100), (103), and (110) for the hexagonal MoSe<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS No. 29-0914). In addition, a peak at ≈7.04° can be observed, which corresponds to expanded (002) plane, exhibiting an obvious shift of 6.66° compared with the JCPDS data. According to the Bragg equation, the interlayer spacing is calculated to be 1.26 nm, indicating formation of an expanded interlayer, which may be attributed to the intercalation of in situ carbon between the interlayers<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0023_R_d256563257e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0036_R_d256563257e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Energy">36</a></span> during the PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor calcination with Se powder at 700 °C. Raman spectra were used to further characterize the MoSe<sub>2</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and carbon materials in the composite. In Raman spectrum of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0001">1</a>C), there are two weak peaks at ≈395 and 515 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which symbolize the B<sup>1</sup><sub>g</sub> and A<sup>1</sup><sub>g</sub> modes of the anatase phase of the TiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0037_R_d256563257e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Phys. Chem. B">37</a></span> Meanwhile, a strong peak at 237.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> is the A<sup>1</sup><sub>g</sub> mode originating from the out-of-plane vibration of the Mo<span class="icomoon"></span>Se band and another peak at ≈289.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> belongs to the E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> mode originating from the Mo<span class="icomoon"></span>Se in-plane vibration.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0014_R_d256563257e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nanoscale">14</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0019_R_d256563257e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a></span> Compared to the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C, slight shifts of the corresponding peaks occurred due to the surface strain that may be caused by the TiO<sub>2</sub> coating.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0038_R_d256563257e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Opt. Express">38</a></span> In addition, two peaks around 1360 and 1591 cm<sup>−1</sup> are observed, corresponding to the D band for defected and disordered carbon and the G band derived from the vibration of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms, respectively.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0014_R_d256563257e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nanoscale">14</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0019_R_d256563257e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0039_R_d256563257e799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></span> The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> intensity ratio is 0.92, higher than 0.90 in the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C, indicating a more defected and disordered structure in the carbon component of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0039_R_d256563257e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></span> As roughly verified by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), as shown in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0001">1</a>D, the mass contents of carbon, MoSe<sub>2</sub>, and TiO<sub>2</sub> in the composite are 15.11, 74.36, and 10.53%, respectively. And the detailed calculating process is added in the Supporting Information. Moreover, the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres exhibit a specific Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of 14.18 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0001">1</a>E), suggesting there are sufficient active sites for the electrolyte contact when used as anode material in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM) images offer insights into the morphology and microstructure of the product. A panoramic view of the product (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs876-fig-0002">2</a>A) indicates that it is composed of uniform nanospheres with size ≈100 nm, which is consistent with the PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor and MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C (Figure S1, Supporting Information). This result reveals that the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> can still maintain the nanosphere without any aggregation or collapse after TiO<sub>2</sub> coating and even high-temperature calcination. Compared with the smooth surface of the PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor and MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C, the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> possesses a rough surface with assembled nanoparticles (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0002">2</a>B), suggesting the successful coating of the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer. It should be noted that the agglomeration of MoSe<sub>2</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> occurs (Figure S2, Supporting Information), indicating the importance of in situ P<span class="icomoon"></span>C on the sphere structure preservation. As can be seen from Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0002">2</a>C, the layered structure and well-defined TiO<sub>2</sub> layers on the surface of the nanospheres can be clearly observed in the low-magnification TEM image. A typical high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image is shown in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0002">2</a>D, from which the expanded (002) lattice spacing (≈1.26 nm) of MoSe<sub>2</sub> can be clearly observed and is consistent with the XRD results. The increased interlayer spacing means lower ion diffusion resistance and more available and accessible active surface area, promoting the rate capability when used as an SIB anode.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0019_R_d256563257e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A">19</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0040_R_d256563257e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a></span> Besides, two distinct lattice fringes at the edge of nanospheres with spacings of 0.35 and 0.23 nm can be observed, respectively, in well agreement with the (101) and (112) planes of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>. The selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0002">2</a>E) shows multiple diffraction rings, confirming the formation of a polycrystalline structure. As shown in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0002">2</a>F–L and Figure S3 (Supporting Information), the high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) image and the corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectrum (EDX) elemental mappings unambiguously identify the Mo, Se, C, and P elements covering the entire spherical structure, while Ti and O elements are mainly dispersed on the surface of the sphere, revealing that the architecture has ingeniously integrated features of the hybrid nanostructures.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs876-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2fa1acd3-1379-4bda-849c-ba67ca33332c/advs876-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2fa1acd3-1379-4bda-849c-ba67ca33332c/advs876-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/355b8dc9-db96-4a48-8466-20f954c16be4/advs876-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2fa1acd3-1379-4bda-849c-ba67ca33332c/advs876-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs876-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and structure of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid nanospheres. A,B) FESEM images. C) TEM images. D) HRTEM image. E) The corresponding SAED pattern. F–L) HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding EDX mappings of Mo, Se, C, P, Ti, and O. The scale bar in (F) also applies to (G)–(L).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs876-fig-0003">3</a>) were recorded to further examine the surface information and oxidation states of the chemical elements in the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres. Six elements, Mo, Se, Ti, O, C, and P, can be clearly seen from the survey spectrum in Figure S4 (Supporting Information). The Mo 3d core level spectrum is shown in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0003">3</a>A, which can be fitted into four peaks at 227.8, 230.9, 232.6, and 235.5 eV. The characteristic peaks at 227.8 and 230.9 eV can be attributed to Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0023_R_d256563257e930" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></span> In addition, the peaks at 232.6 and 235.5 eV represent the appearance of Mo<sup>6+</sup> due to the partial oxidization of MoSe<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0041_R_d256563257e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Phys. Chem. C">41</a></span> In the Se 3d spectrum (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0003">3</a>B), there are two peaks located at 53.1 and 54.0 eV, which are assigned, respectively, to the Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0042_R_d256563257e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">42</a></span> Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0003">3</a>C depicts the Ti 2p core level spectrum with two peaks centered at 458.1 eV for Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 463.9 eV for Ti 2p<sub>5/2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0043_R_d256563257e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Sol. Energy">43</a></span> The distance between the Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> core levels is 5.8 eV, suggesting a normal state of Ti<sup>4+</sup> in the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0033_R_d256563257e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Sci. Rep.">33</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0044_R_d256563257e965" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Energy Environ. Sci.">44</a></span> As shown in the O 1s spectrum (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0003">3</a>D), the binding energy of Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O, O<span class="icomoon"></span>H, and H<sub>2</sub>O is observed at 529.8, 531.9, and 533.8 eV, respectively.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0045_R_d256563257e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Catal. Commun.">45</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0046_R_d256563257e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nanoscale">46</a></span> These results indicate the successful coating of the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> phase. The XPS spectrum of the P 2p core-level (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0003">3</a>E) displays two peaks at 133.0 and 135.1 eV, which are related to P<span class="icomoon"></span>C and PO bonds, respectively.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0011_R_d256563257e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0047_R_d256563257e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Nano">47</a></span> The C 1s spectrum (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0003">3</a>F) could be divided into three peaks, which are located at 284.3, 285.1, and 285.7 eV, corresponding to sp<sup>2</sup> C, sp<sup>3</sup> C, and C<span class="icomoon"></span>P bonds, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs876-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7fece81d-d9a7-4b2e-8d0e-fd6614ccd88f/advs876-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7fece81d-d9a7-4b2e-8d0e-fd6614ccd88f/advs876-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/88902ff5-7f5a-4f77-a7ff-aaf96d7cfb06/advs876-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7fece81d-d9a7-4b2e-8d0e-fd6614ccd88f/advs876-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs876-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS spectra of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> composite. A–F) High-resolution XPS spectra for Mo 3d, Se 3d, Ti 2p, O 1s, P 2p, and C 1s.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The successful synthesis of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid nanospheres for a superior SIB anode material is confirmed from the excellent electrochemical behavior (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs876-fig-0004">4</a>). Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0004">4</a>A shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> anode in a voltage range of 0.5–3.0 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> for the initial five cycles. During the first discharge process, three main cathodic peaks at 1.53, 0.60, and 0.50 V can be observed. Compared with CV curves of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C and TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure S5, Supporting Information), the peak at 1.53 V corresponds to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> phase.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0048_R_d256563257e1037" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a></span> The reduction peak at 0.60 eV can be attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the interlayer of MoSe<sub>2</sub> and formation of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoSe<sub>2</sub>, and the other peak at 0.50 eV is assigned to conversion reaction from Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>MoSe<sub>2</sub> to Mo metal and Na<sub>2</sub>Se with the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0023_R_d256563257e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0049_R_d256563257e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a></span> Accordingly, the oxidation peaks at 1.67 and 2.10 V are associated with the oxidation of Mo to MoSe<sub>2</sub> and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiO<sub>2</sub> to TiO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. After the first cycle, the following CV curves are almost overlapped and the reduction peaks move to higher potential, indicating the good reversibility of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> anode during the repeated sodiation/desodiation processes. The main peak locating at 1.38 V should be attributed to the reactions of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion, the interlayer of MoSe<sub>2</sub>, and subsequent reduction into Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>Se.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs876-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/036706cb-8e23-4250-91f1-daeac2c5e974/advs876-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/036706cb-8e23-4250-91f1-daeac2c5e974/advs876-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/48b63033-b94b-47c0-af27-7db0500086a2/advs876-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/036706cb-8e23-4250-91f1-daeac2c5e974/advs876-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs876-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. A) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. B) Typical charge–discharge profiles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. C,D) Cycling performance at 5.0 and 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. E) Rate cycling behavior.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0004">4</a>B exhibits the galvanostatic discharge–charge curves of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> composite electrode at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.5–3.0 V, which are well consistent with the CV results. The initial charge and discharge capacities are ≈293.3 and 360.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, giving an ICE of 81.3%. As the cycles proceed, the charge and discharge capacities from 10th to 100th cycles show a trend of decrease followed by a slight increase in subsequent cycles. This result is further demonstrated by a galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling test, as shown in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0004">4</a>C,D. The reversible discharge capacities for the 500th, 800th, and 1000th cycles are 214, 223, and 229 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with average CEs up to ≈100%, showing an outstanding cycling stability. The cycling performance of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C and TiO<sub>2</sub> is also carried out at the current of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0004">4</a>C), which clearly demonstrates the effects of the TiO<sub>2</sub> modified MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C in improving the sodium storage performance. It is found that although the reversible capacity of TiO<sub>2</sub> is noticeably low in the voltage range of 0.5–3.0 V, its cycling stability is clearly better than the other two materials. The MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C electrode shows higher discharge capacities than those of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> in the initial 40 cycles. After 40 cycles, it exhibits a rapid decrease and stabilizes the discharge capacity of only 138 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 8000 cycles with a low capacity retention of 50.7% in comparison with the value for the second cycle. And the capacities of MoSe<sub>2</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> are lower and a capacity of 60.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be maintained after 8000 cycles. In contrast, the reversible capacity of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode decreases slightly in the initial 243 cycles because of the polarization when cycled at high current and delivers a little increase capacity attributed to progressive kinetic activation in the electrode.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0012_R_d256563257e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0031_R_d256563257e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0040_R_d256563257e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0048_R_d256563257e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a></span> After those, a stable capacity of 214 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be delivered after 8000 cycles, giving a capacity retention of 82.9%. This result indicates that MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, as expected, exhibits a synthetic effect combining the advantages for the individual components. Moreover, long cycling life can be verified as shown in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0004">4</a>D. Even at a high current density of 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the discharge capacity of 154 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be maintained over 10 000 cycles with CEs as high as 100%, delivering a capacity retention of 68.1%.</p>
         
         <p>As expected, the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid electrode manifests an excellent high rate capability, as indicated in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0004">4</a>E. At current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacities of the hybrid nanospheres are ≈270, 250, 240, 236, 228, and 214 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at the high current densities of 10.0 and 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the hybrid can still deliver capacities as high as 202 and 175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. This high-rate sodium-storage capability exceeds those of many other MoS<sub>2</sub>/MoSe<sub>2</sub>-based nanostructures such as MoS<sub>2</sub>:C nanotube, MoS<sub>2</sub>/C hybrid, MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflowers, PEO-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanocomposites, C-MoSe<sub>2</sub>/rGO composite, and few-layered and carbon-modified MoSe<sub>2</sub> nanotube (Figure S6, Supporting Information). More importantly, when the rate is gradually decreased back to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity can recover to 293 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which could originate from the reactivation process caused by high-rate sodiation.</p>
         
         <p>To explore the underlying reason of the long cycling and ultrafast sodium storage of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, the redox pseudocapacitive contribution in the hybrid electrode was examined by separating the capacitive capacity and the diffusion-controlled capacity. The reaction kinetics of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres with Na<sup>+</sup> ions can be investigated by CV profiles at different sweep rates (ν) from 0.5 to 2.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. As displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs876-fig-0005">5</a>A, all the CV curves have similar peak shapes except for the corresponding shift during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes. The total stored charges contributed by diffusion-controlled or/and capacitive contributions can be divided using the power law <i>i</i> = <i>aν<sup>b</sup></i>,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0024_R_d256563257e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0040_R_d256563257e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0049_R_d256563257e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0050_R_d256563257e1242" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Phys. Chem. C">50</a></span> where ν is the scan rate, and both <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters. Generally, <i>b</i> = 0.5 indicates a diffusion-controlled process, and <i>b</i> = 1.0 represents capacitive-contributed charge storage. Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0005">5</a>B shows the slope of the corresponding log (ν) versus log (<i>i</i>) plots, in which the <i>b</i>-values are 0.98 and 0.94 for cathodic and anodic peaks, respectively. These values suggest that the two mechanisms coexist in the sodiation/desodiation process, which is mainly controlled by the capacitive process. Moreover, the contributions from the two mechanisms can be estimated from the formula <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>) = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> at a fixed potential.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0011_R_d256563257e1272" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0049_R_d256563257e1272" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">49</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0050_R_d256563257e1272" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Phys. Chem. C">50</a></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0005">5</a>C, 86.2% of the total capacity is attributed to the capacitive contribution at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Along with the sweep rate increases, the capacitance contribution gradually increases, reaching a value of 92.8% at 2.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#advs876-fig-0005">5</a>D). The kinetics analyses obviously manifest the gradual increase of capacity contribution, where the fast electrochemical kinetics of involved surficial reactions contribute to the high rate capability. The improved Na<sup>+</sup>-reaction kinetics of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> anode material are further supported by the lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> than that of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C electrode (Figure S7A, Supporting Information) and the decreasing charge-transfer resistance upon cycling (Figure S7B, Supporting Information). Postmortem study verifies that the structure of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid spheres can be well retained after 1000 cycles at a current density of 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S8, Supporting Information). This confirms TiO<sub>2</sub> coating can relieve structural collapse and promote ultralong cycling stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs876-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f5c3b3c2-b444-483c-9a18-6297cb021d7b/advs876-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f5c3b3c2-b444-483c-9a18-6297cb021d7b/advs876-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ba6a6caa-d377-4673-b8d2-01ce681be904/advs876-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f5c3b3c2-b444-483c-9a18-6297cb021d7b/advs876-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs876-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetics analysis of the sodium storage behavior for the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. A) CV curves at different scan rates. B) <i>b</i>-value analysis based on the relationship between the peak currents and the scan rates. C) Capacitive (shade region) and diffusion-controlled charge storage contributions for the CV curve at scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. D) Contribution ratio of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled charges at different scan rates.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In view of the superior sodium-storage performance of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> in a half cell, full cells are further assembled with homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO4)<sub>3</sub>@C (NVP@C) as the cathode material to evaluate its potential for practical application. The homemade NVP@C was synthesized by a hydrothermal assisted sol-gel method,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0051_R_d256563257e1340" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - J. Mater. Chem. A">51</a></span> which has good phase purity and crystal structure as shown in Figure S9A (Supporting Information). When used as cathode material for a half cell, it exhibits a capacity of 80.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles and shows a flat voltage plateau around 3.4 V (Figure S9B,C, Supporting Information), which is consistent with previous reports.<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0009_R_d256563257e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Nano">9</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0026_R_d256563257e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Nano">26</a>, <a href="#advs876-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0052_R_d256563257e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Mater.">52</a></span> For a sodium-ion full battery, a discharge voltage plateau around 1.85 V can be seen from the galvanostatic discharge–charge curves at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.5–3.0 V (Figure S10A, Supporting Information). Figure S10B (Supporting Information) shows the cycling stability of the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>//NVP@C full cells at current density of 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. A reversible capacity of 242.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can be maintained after 100 cycles with the CE over 99%, suggesting a robust cyclability of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> when applied into full cells.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs876-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs876-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a facile self-templating strategy has been developed to synthesize PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres using PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> as the precursor via a hydrolysis reaction. Through a subsequent simultaneous selenization–carbonization process, MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid nanospheres can be easily achieved. Benefiting from the unique structural and multicompositional features, the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid nanospheres provide superior sodium-storage performance based on high capacity, excellent cycling stability, and exceptional rate capability. Specifically, the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> hybrid nanospheres deliver a reversible capacity of 214 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 8000 cycles and 154 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 10000 cycles as well as an ultrahigh rate capability up to 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity of 175 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. This work could open up a new avenue to design and prepare complex multicompositional hybrids toward various applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs876-sec-0040">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs876-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> Precursor</i>: According to a previous report,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0047_R_d256563257e1423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Nano">47</a></span> the synthetic method is as follows. Pyrrole solution (420 µL in 25 mL of absolute ethanol) was added dropwise to an aqueous solution of phosphomolybdic acid (H<sub>3</sub>PMo<sub>12</sub>O<sub>40</sub>·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O, 2.19 g in 100 mL of deionized H<sub>2</sub>O). After the mixture was continuously stirred at room temperature for 12 h and then aged for 24 h, the product was obtained through centrifugation, washed with deionized H<sub>2</sub>O and absolute ethanol several times, and then dried at 60 °C. The PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor was collected for further use.</p>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanospheres</i>: In a typical procedure,<span><a href="#advs876-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs876-bib-0035_R_d256563257e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Chem. - Eur. J.">35</a></span> first, 200 mg of the above PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor was dispersed in 96.7 mL 0.1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> HCl solution which contains 2 g dihydroxybis (ammonium lactato) titanium (IV) (50 wt% solution in H<sub>2</sub>O) under magnetic stirring at room temperature for 2 h. The PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were collected by centrifugation, washed with deionized H<sub>2</sub>O and absolute ethanol several times, and then dried at 60 °C. The PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> (100 mg) was placed in a porcelain boat at the downstream side of the furnace with 200 mg of Se power placed another boat at the upstream side in the tube furnace. Eventually, the MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanospheres were obtained after heated at 700 °C with a scan rate of 1 °C min<sup>−1</sup> in a H<sub>2</sub>/Ar flow for 2 h. The MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C nanospheres were prepared in the same condition except the TiO<sub>2</sub> coating. And TiO<sub>2</sub> was prepared in a similar procedure to MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> except the addition of PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> precursor and calcination with Se powder. MoSe<sub>2</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> was prepared using a similar procedure to MoSe<sub>2</sub>/P-C@TiO<sub>2</sub> except the calcination of PPy-PMo<sub>12</sub> at 600 °C in air before calcination with Se powder in order to remove the carbon source of PPy.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterizations</i>: XRD patterns were performed on a Rigaku UItima IV X-Ray Diffractometer. FESEM images were recorded by a scanning electron microscope (Philips XL30 FEG SEM). TEM, HRTEM, and HAADF-STEM images were collected on FEI Talos F200X TEM at 200 kV. Raman spectra were carried out on a HORIBA Evolution Ramanscope with the excitation wavelength of 532 nm. TGA was carried out using PerkinElmer TGA 7 in an air atmosphere with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. XPS was measured by Thermo ESCALAB 250 surface analysis system. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isothermal curve and the BET surface area was calculated via Micro ASAP2020 at 77 K.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The performances of sodium storage were evaluated by coin cells (CR2032) assembled in an Ar glovebox with a voltage window of 0.5–3.0 V. The sodium metal, glass fiber, and 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> solution in diethyleneglycoldimethylether (DEGDME) played the parts of counter electrode, separator, and electrolyte, respectively. The working electrode was prepared by forming a H<sub>2</sub>O-based slurry composed of active material, carbon black, and sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) with a weight ratio of 6:2:2 on a copper foil. Then the coated foil was dried at 80 °C and cut into 12 mm discs with a loading of ≈1.0 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. CV curves and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were performed using a Gamry 30115 electrochemical workstation with different scan rates from 0.5 to 2.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup> and frequencies of 0.1 MHz to 10 mHz. Galvanostatic discharge/charge cycling was carried out using Neware-5 V10 mA system (Shenzhen Xinwei). For Na-ion full cells, the cathode was made of homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C, carbon black, and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) were mixed in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) with a weight ratio of 8:1:1 to form a slurry which was coated on an aluminum foil.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs876-sec-0050-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
